<div id="review_2405229727" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2405229727" />
    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2405229727?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=bio-memoir">bio-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=bestest">bestest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=2-asia">2-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=woman-authors">woman-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=war-genocide">war-genocide</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2405229727" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer970380535295826140"><blockquote>
  <i>“I still remember the moment when my gaze fell upon the mutilated face of a young woman, her features slashed through with a bayonet. Soundlessly, and without fuss, some tender thing deep inside me broke. Something that, until then, I hadn't realised was there.” </i>
  <br/>
</blockquote><br/>A semi-fictional account of unnecessarily violent supression of a student uprising in Han Kang's home town, Gwangju, South Korea in 1980 through point of view of inter-related characters. I guess it would have been brutal to expec</span>
  <span id="freeText970380535295826140" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“I still remember the moment when my gaze fell upon the mutilated face of a young woman, her features slashed through with a bayonet. Soundlessly, and without fuss, some tender thing deep inside me broke. Something that, until then, I hadn't realised was there.” </i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />A semi-fictional account of unnecessarily violent supression of a student uprising in Han Kang's home town, Gwangju, South Korea in 1980 through point of view of inter-related characters. I guess it would have been brutal to expect another 'The Vegetarian' from her but this is beautiful in its own way - showing what it means having to live through such incidences - how it changes the way one sees the world:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“Is it true that human beings are fundamentally cruel? Is the experience of cruelty the only thing we share as a species? Is the dignity that we cling to nothing but self-delusion, masking from ourselves the single truth: that each one of us is capable of being reduced to an insect, a ravening beast, a lump of meat? To be degraded, slaughtered - is this the essential of humankind, one which history has confirmed as inevitable?”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br /> what it means to lose someone dear to illogical brutalities of psychopaths that seem to be getting hold of power everywhere - that is, to lose them so entirely both body and soul taken away from you (there must be something soothing for a grieving person in the acts of last rites, something that helps them to come to terms with their loss - and several mothers were deprived of that 'something'):<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“After you died I could not hold a funeral,<br />And so my life became a funeral.”<br /><br />“After you were lost to us, all our hours declined into evening.<br />Evening are our streets and our houses.<br />In this half-light that no longer darkens nor lightens, we eat, and walk, and sleep.”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />and how survival in such cases is just a relative term - how you come out of such things different, broken irreparably (remember Headth Ledger's 'Whatever doesn't kill you, makes you stranger.")<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“I'm fighting alone, every day. I fight with the hell that I survived. I fight with the fact of my own humanity. I fight with the idea that death is the only way of escaping this fact.”<br /><br />“Some memories never heal. Rather than fading with the passage of time, those memories become the only things that are left behind when all else is abraded. The world darkens, like electric bulbs going out one by one. I am aware that I am not a safe person.” <i><br /></i></i>
</blockquote><br />*****<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“Glass is transparent, right? And fragile. That's the fundamental nature of glass. And that's why objects that are made of glass have to be handled with care. After all, if they end up smashed or cracked or chipped, then they're good for nothing, right, you just have to chuck them away.<br />Before, we used to have a kind of glass that couldn't be broken. A truth so hard and clear it might as well have been made of glass. So when you think about it, it was only when we were shattered that we proved we had souls. That what we really were was humans made of glass.” </i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="970380535295826140" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1796249276" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1796249276" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1796249276?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of fiction based on real events and gorgeous writing</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1796249276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1207440073935969924">First and foremost, everyone who loves beautiful writing should read this book. That being said, results may vary. My idea of beautiful may not be your idea of beautiful. This book contains disturbing imagery described passionately. Kang finds beauty in even the ugliest places. <br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30091914.Human_Acts' title='Human Acts by Han Kang' rel='nofollow noopener'>Human Acts</a> is a breath of fresh air after Kang's disappointing <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25489025.The_Vegetarian' title='The Vegetarian by Han Kang' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Vegetarian</a>. I give every author a second chance, no matter how much I despise the first story I read from them. Sometimes, it works out in my favor. This </span>
  <span id="freeText1207440073935969924" style="display:none">First and foremost, everyone who loves beautiful writing should read this book. That being said, results may vary. My idea of beautiful may not be your idea of beautiful. This book contains disturbing imagery described passionately. Kang finds beauty in even the ugliest places. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30091914.Human_Acts" title="Human Acts by Han Kang" rel="nofollow noopener">Human Acts</a> is a breath of fresh air after Kang's disappointing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25489025.The_Vegetarian" title="The Vegetarian by Han Kang" rel="nofollow noopener">The Vegetarian</a>. I give every author a second chance, no matter how much I despise the first story I read from them. Sometimes, it works out in my favor. This was one of those times. <br /><br />This is the first mosaic novel I've enjoyed. Mainly because I never know, going in, that these novels are mosaics. Many of these books are marketed as novels, when, in fact, they are collections of connected short stories. If I go into a novel wanting a novel, I want a novel, not a short story collection. Nothing wrong with collections. I've published three myself, so far be it from me to knock the format. But I want to know if that's what I'm reading. Sometimes I feel like a nut, sometimes I don't.<br /><br /><i>The Vegetarian</i> was also written in this mosaic style, with alternating POVs, with a different character in every chapter, so I'm assuming that is just this author's style. Here we find everything from the rarely-well-done second-person POV to the little-used epistolary format to the commonly-used first-person perspective. I dug how Kang juggled the styles here. The switches didn't feel near as jarring as they did in <i>The Vegetarian</i>, and I actually cared about the people in this book, whereas her last novel was plagued by an entire cast of unlikable assholes who were not given near enough time to develop. I'm all for stories about irredeemable folks, but give me a dog to care about, or something. <br /><br />The book has one chapter in particular that resonated with me and it might have affected my rating by a star. Meaning, I'm giving this novel five stars based on one 30-page chapter. If the second part didn't exist, this would be a solid four-star read. But "The Boy's Friend" pushed this into perfect-territory for me. Although they might exist outside of this novel, I've never before read a sequence of events in such a way. I was awestruck by the delivery. Probably in my top ten best written chapters of all time. <br /><br />In summation: Everybody has a bad day. Judging them solely based on one awful outing is short-sighted. I'm glad I gave Kang another chance. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30091914.Human_Acts" title="Human Acts by Han Kang" rel="nofollow noopener">Human Acts</a> was just what I was looking for at this moment in my life. All five stars are, without a doubt in my mind, deserved. Thanks to Crown Publishing for thus far providing me with two of this author's works, free of charge, in return for unbiased reviews. <br /><br /><b>Final Judgment: Timely and poignant, especially in today's political climate.<br /><br /></b></span>
  <a data-text-id="1207440073935969924" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2320841490" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2320841490" />
    <a title="Supreeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64325592-supreeth"><img alt="Supreeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602673666p2/64325592.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2320841490?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Supreeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Supreeth" href="/user/show/64325592-supreeth">Supreeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781783781621" href="/book/show/36535729-human-acts">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64325592-supreeth?shelf=korean">korean</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64325592-supreeth?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64325592-supreeth?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64325592-supreeth?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2320841490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3621212594858269310">So, hundredth read of the year!<br/>-----------<br/><b>Background:</b><br/><br/>On may 18, 1980 South Korean citizens saw an opportunity to end their non-democratized government when military government was forced. Students from Chonnam University were the first bunch of people to initiate the protest, followed by other students and people all over the town. They started robbing government offices and police stations. As a counter strike, military fired arms and killed around six hundred citizens making that uprising one </span>
  <span id="freeText3621212594858269310" style="display:none">So, hundredth read of the year!<br />-----------<br /><b>Background:</b><br /><br />On may 18, 1980 South Korean citizens saw an opportunity to end their non-democratized government when military government was forced. Students from Chonnam University were the first bunch of people to initiate the protest, followed by other students and people all over the town. They started robbing government offices and police stations. As a counter strike, military fired arms and killed around six hundred citizens making that uprising one of the worst in South Korea's history. The atrocities performed by the military is inhuman, therefore 'human acts'.<br /><br /><b>Human Acts:</b><br /><br />At first glance this book might just seem be an excuse to bring up this issue — the sins of the past — in fictional form. If you've read Vegetarian by Han Kang before, you're both satisfied and disappointed at the same time. Human acts deviates from the Vegetarian in a way, but not too far, Han Kang still writes about the dark side of the human nature. The event mentioned above is unfolded through the voices of the victims — a boy who was shot, his mates at protest, women who face the aftermath of protest, the editors, and the people whose lives are still affected by that event. Prose is poetic, typical Han Kang; the translation is just so perfect that I don't have to write about the hackneyed translation like I do all the time. I seriously believe that translation justifies the original script. The prose wanders from first person narratives to second to third person, including a chapter narrated by the dead body. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“After you died I could not hold a funeral,<br />And so my life became a funeral.” </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Some parts of the the book were traumatic, especially the violence against women, but Kang's beautiful writing makes up for it. I hope we get to read more of her.<br />This one's really important.<br /><br /> <i>
  <a href="https://disapppearhere.wordpress.com/2018/10/22/human-acts-by-han-kang-a-short-review/" rel="nofollow noopener">also on blog</a>
</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="3621212594858269310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1894140597" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1894140597" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3386994-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483414253p2/3386994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1894140597?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/3386994-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

Even though the book is translated, the rhythm and flow are beautiful!</span>

    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1627680401?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1627680401" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10772280433289536972">I enjoyed reading and discussing <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25489025.The_Vegetarian' title='The Vegetarian by Han Kang' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Vegetarian</a> by Han Kang, so I went looking for her other novel that had been translated into English. This one is of a completely different tone - following a handful of people during the events of the Gwangju Uprising (AKA Gwangju Massacre, depending on who is discussing it) in 1980, and its aftermath. Kang was born in Gwangju and moved away in 1979 (but she appears to live there now), so this book is inspired by the story surrounding her childhood, of people</span>
  <span id="freeText10772280433289536972" style="display:none">I enjoyed reading and discussing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25489025.The_Vegetarian" title="The Vegetarian by Han Kang" rel="nofollow noopener">The Vegetarian</a> by Han Kang, so I went looking for her other novel that had been translated into English. This one is of a completely different tone - following a handful of people during the events of the Gwangju Uprising (AKA Gwangju Massacre, depending on who is discussing it) in 1980, and its aftermath. Kang was born in Gwangju and moved away in 1979 (but she appears to live there now), so this book is inspired by the story surrounding her childhood, of people who she and her family knew. <br /><br />Probably not surprisingly, I had never heard of this event, and had only recently heard of Gwangju because a former student worker moved there to teach English. But we had people from South Korea visiting us regularly, a minister who ran an orphanage in Seoul. Perhaps he like those living in the book considered Gwangju as irrelevant to Seoul, or he had other priorities, but I remember more about his stories of his Dad being killed by the Chinese than I ever heard stories like this.<br /><br />The historical events of the Gwangju Uprising have been covered substantially in Korean film and literature. The introduction to this novel (by the translator) explains that Kang wanted to do something differently. No heroics, only corpses. It is indeed graphic in the sense of dead bodies, horrific in the sense that characters can do little to escape it. There is even a character that feels like foreshadowing of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25489025.The_Vegetarian" title="The Vegetarian by Han Kang" rel="nofollow noopener">The Vegetarian</a>, one who starts being sickened by meat and refuses to eat it. It made me wonder if the roots of that novel are found here; if the character of Yeong-hye has more than nightmares in her memory....<br /><br /><i>I was able to access a review copy of this book from the publisher through Edelweiss, in exchange for an honest review. The book actually came out in January in English, so they may just be promoting it after the success of The Vegetarian (which is on the shortlist for the Man Booker International Prize.)</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10772280433289536972" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16085857812976775584">Human Acts is a novel based on the true events of the Gwangju massacre of 1980 (which I knew nothing about before starting this book). It is told from the perspective of different people involved in the violently suppressed uprising, and centres especially around the middle school student Dong-Ho who we meet in the first chapter, when he is looking for his missing friend.<br/><br/>The language used in this novel is starkly beautiful and poetic, and in some ways that makes its unflinching telling of this h</span>
  <span id="freeText16085857812976775584" style="display:none">Human Acts is a novel based on the true events of the Gwangju massacre of 1980 (which I knew nothing about before starting this book). It is told from the perspective of different people involved in the violently suppressed uprising, and centres especially around the middle school student Dong-Ho who we meet in the first chapter, when he is looking for his missing friend.<br /><br />The language used in this novel is starkly beautiful and poetic, and in some ways that makes its unflinching telling of this horrifically violent and senseless period of Korean history all the more disturbing and upsetting. <br /><br />This is a deeply profound and sombre book that will stay with me for a very long time. Highly, highly recommended.</span>

<div id="review_1821393862" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1821393862" />
    <a title="Ellie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2908629-ellie"><img alt="Ellie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399799985p2/2908629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1821393862?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellie" href="/user/show/2908629-ellie">Ellie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846275968" href="/book/show/28172483-human-acts">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=socio-political">socio-political</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=2016indchallenge">2016indchallenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1821393862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1222018219369762801">“…if we can only keep our eyes open, if we can all hold our gazes steady, until the bitter end…”<br/><br/>This line appears toward the end of Han Kang’s (author of The Vegetarian) new book, Human Acts. And certainly Kang has an amazing ability to gaze steadily at painful material, as witnessed in both her books.<br/><br/>The pain in Human Acts is different than that of The Vegetarian. In The Vegetarian, we followed the suffering of one woman, in Human Acts, that of thousands. The book is based on the Gwangju uprisi</span>
  <span id="freeText1222018219369762801" style="display:none">“…if we can only keep our eyes open, if we can all hold our gazes steady, until the bitter end…”<br /><br />This line appears toward the end of Han Kang’s (author of The Vegetarian) new book, Human Acts. And certainly Kang has an amazing ability to gaze steadily at painful material, as witnessed in both her books.<br /><br />The pain in Human Acts is different than that of The Vegetarian. In The Vegetarian, we followed the suffering of one woman, in Human Acts, that of thousands. The book is based on the Gwangju uprising (in South Korea) in 1980 and the massacre of its participants. We see the event through the eyes of a young boy, Dong-ho, his grieving mother, his friend, a woman who “survived” (the survivors are all so traumatized by their experience that the word “survival” seems almost meaningless; although they are physically alive, they are shattered), and a contemporary poet who is researching the event.<br /><br />The brutal events are told with writing so beautiful it renders the harshness of the events even more vividly. There are repeated images of brokenness (“Soundlessly, and without fuss, some tender thing deep inside me broke. Something that, until then, I hadn’t even realized was there,” and “…it was only when we were shattered that we proved we had souls. That what we really were was humans made of glass.”).The innocence of the young dissidents, students not only at the university but high schools, even middle schools, the beauty of Kang’s lyrical descriptions of their idealism contrasts stunningly with their vicious slaughter and, still worse, the torture of those who “survived” the initial attack. One of the survivors writes of “that moment when the contours of suffering coalesce into clarity, a clarity colder and harder than any nightmare could ever be.”<br /><br />Kang’s writing is filled with moments of this kind of clarity. It is often a difficult and painful book to read. It is terrifying to see what humans can do to each other. There are the human acts of resistence and the inhuman-and yet, still, somehow, human-acts that are brutally perpetrated by one person onto another. There were times I could hardly bear to go on and moments when it seemed that the courage of the dissidents was turned into some kind of cruel joke in the light of their merciless treatment.<br /><br />Did their suffering serve any purpose? Did it in any way help end the dictatorship? One of the characters remembers how a leader kept repeating “We are noble” but this nobility seems pale in comparison to the suffering that ensues.<br /><br />While not a book for the faint-hearted, Human Acts seems to me to be a necessary one to read, to honor those who fight oppression everywhere, to those who die unknown and unacknowledged or live scarred and suffering. Kang has created a vivid depiction of a doomed uprising. It is a brave and wonderful work.<br /><br />I want to thank LibraryThing and Han Kang for making this book available to me in exchange for an honest review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1222018219369762801" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11181627302653216656">When I finished this book almost a month ago, I thought it was a solid 4 star read, not one of my favourite ever books, but still great. Since then I've often found myself remembering certain scenes and reminding myself that I read them in this book, not in another much loved classic. I think it's because this feels like something I've read before, while at the same time, it's not like anything else that I've encountered. As I found in The Vegetarian, both the writing and translation were fantas</span>
  <span id="freeText11181627302653216656" style="display:none">When I finished this book almost a month ago, I thought it was a solid 4 star read, not one of my favourite ever books, but still great. Since then I've often found myself remembering certain scenes and reminding myself that I read them in this book, not in another much loved classic. I think it's because this feels like something I've read before, while at the same time, it's not like anything else that I've encountered. As I found in The Vegetarian, both the writing and translation were fantastic. In fact I find it difficult to fault at all. 4.5 stars, rounding up to 5. </span>

<div id="review_1609109735" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1609109735" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4934590-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425387897p2/4934590.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1609109735?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/4934590-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846275968" href="/book/show/28172483-human-acts">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1609109735" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1436663996849830027">The writing is beautiful and the translation assured. It follows several people in the aftermath of the Gwangju uprising and subsequent quelling by the army. The aftermath creeps across the years as dark tendrils that still lay hold of those involved. <br/><br/>Sounds like a compelling plot as Kang plays witness to the events of 1980. But these are all bookish quotes objectively examining this second translated work from Kang that makes The Vegetarian seem like a happy fairy tale.<br/><br/>But it had such a profoun</span>
  <span id="freeText1436663996849830027" style="display:none">The writing is beautiful and the translation assured. It follows several people in the aftermath of the Gwangju uprising and subsequent quelling by the army. The aftermath creeps across the years as dark tendrils that still lay hold of those involved. <br /><br />Sounds like a compelling plot as Kang plays witness to the events of 1980. But these are all bookish quotes objectively examining this second translated work from Kang that makes The Vegetarian seem like a happy fairy tale.<br /><br />But it had such a profound effect on me. I remember visiting South Korea with my family in the late 80’s on vacation. I’m Canadian born and raised with parents that embrace and love their lives here in Canada - perhaps at the expense of a deliberate forgetting, if not tight-lipped stoicism of their pasts in Korea. I remember standing in a train station in Gwangju and seeing plastered on columns everywhere photos of the aftermath. These weren’t the photojournalism shots we see of sweeping vistas of destruction taken from a remove, anonymous bodies strewn on a dusty roadway. They were almost pornographic. Close up shots of just what remained of a face, now looking remarkably like a halloween mask, the skull completely staved in from repeated bludgeoning. Gore and viscera displayed in a public transit station, multiple strangers in death for all to examine. Closely. Maybe that’s what was in the chapbooks referenced in the book. I see them in my head now. <br /><br />I’m a tourist. I’ve spent maybe a year total in South Korea. I have only the most rudimentary understanding of the language. I live a privileged, suburban, middle-class existence so take all my hand-wringing as me-too relevance seeking. <br /><br />I’ve taught Dong-ho and kids his age in South Korea in small towns. I’ve been in the concrete sheds that pass for gymnasiums and walked the dirt packed floors of regional office buildings. I’ve passed by open fields where communities still burn all their garbage. I’ve witnessed the intense physicality of Koreans so at odds with the expansive space of Canada and personal boundaries. <br /><br />And though I’ve never seen it I can imagine them all dead too. I can imagine 15 year old boys tending to hundreds of dead bodies with a matter of fact resolve. I can imagine teachers tortured, struck and humiliated. I can see them just as easily being the ones torturing, hitting and humiliating. In a country where every male has mandatory military service I see only a thin line between tortured and torturer. <br /><br />And it just wrecks me. That’s what a good writer is supposed to evoke but I can’t call what this brings up as enjoyable. Kang dredges up so much of what is ugly and distorted and lays it out in a joyless manner and dares you to look away. I see little in the way of hope, and maybe that’s more honest. If you liked The Vegetarian you’re going to love Human Acts. It just leaves me cold. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1436663996849830027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16655990353030197567" style="display:none">My favorite of January 2017 is a hard call: It’s already turning out to be a fantastic reading year, and Mustafa Khalifa’s The Shell, a story of imprisonment in Syria (Jan 2017) dovetails in startling ways with Han Kang’s novel about the 1980 Gwangju Uprising and subsequent massacre, and then the fallout over the next thirty years, traced through the bodies of different characters. Like Kang’s Man Booker International-winning The Vegetarian (also trans. Smith), this is a novel about the human body, and what makes it, or doesn’t make it, human. A must-read.<br /><br />–M Lynx Qualey<br /><br />from The Best Books We Read In January 2017: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2017/02/01/riot-round-best-books-read-january-2017/">http://bookriot.com/2017/02/01/riot-r...</a> <br />____________________<br /><br /><br />Set during a violent student uprising in South Korea, this is the story of the death of a young boy named Dong-ho; of the heartbreak and hopelessness felt by the oppressed; and of humankind’s eternal struggle for justice and peace. Kang has once again painted a brutal and beautiful portrait of violence and love among people trying to find their voices. It’s a raw, affecting novel.<br /><br />Backlist bump: The Vegetarian by Han Kang<br /><br /><br />Tune in to our weekly podcast dedicated to all things new books, All The Books: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/listen/shows/allthebooks/">http://bookriot.com/listen/shows/allt...</a></span>

<div id="review_1823272045" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1823272045" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2228181-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507660688p2/2228181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1823272045?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/2228181-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846275968" href="/book/show/28172483-human-acts">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2228181-jill?shelf=novels-in-translation">novels-in-translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1823272045" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12352190239065849424">Is it true that human beings are fundamentally cruel? Or, in the words of one of the characters in Human Acts, “To be degraded, damaged, slaughtered – is this the essential fate of humankind, one that history has confirmed as inevitable?”<br/><br/>It’s a dark view, but for those who survived the Gwangju Uprising of 1980, it would appear that cruelty is, indeed, part of being human. As happens all too often in history, laborers and students rose up against a dictatorship and later were arrested or massacre</span>
  <span id="freeText12352190239065849424" style="display:none">Is it true that human beings are fundamentally cruel? Or, in the words of one of the characters in Human Acts, “To be degraded, damaged, slaughtered – is this the essential fate of humankind, one that history has confirmed as inevitable?”<br /><br />It’s a dark view, but for those who survived the Gwangju Uprising of 1980, it would appear that cruelty is, indeed, part of being human. As happens all too often in history, laborers and students rose up against a dictatorship and later were arrested or massacred. As a result, in Korea, the name Gwangju is synonymous with “for whatever is forcibly isolated, beaten down, and brutalized, for all that has been mutilated beyond repair.”<br /><br />The novel, told in six parts and encompassing 35 years, shows how this act – like radiation – continues to affect the lives of those who remain. In the first chapter, a 15-year-old boy named Dong-ho futilely searches for his best friend Jeong-dae. Soon we learn that Dong-ho is yet another victim, with his soul staring into his own body’s dead eyes. The massacre is played back by a female editor who was tortured, by a factory worker who was brutalized, and, in one of the most emotionally haunting sections, by Dong-ho’s mother, who heartbreakingly recalls his earliest years. Han Kang becomes her own character in the epilogue, revealing her own reasons for feeling compelled to tell the story.<br /><br />Reading Human Acts in close proximity to Han Kang’s award-winning novel The Vegetarian, it’s easy to detect certain similarities: the unrelenting darkness of the vision, the multiple narrators, the glimpse into acts of resistance, the gradual insights as the big picture emerges. Yet while The Vegetarian was psychologically intimate, Human Acts is broader, dealing not with a family’s trauma, but an entire country’s. As a result, it transcends The Vegetarian’s theme of “what does it mean to be human” and instead, focuses on “what does it mean to transcend our human condition and make the corpses we sing over into something more than just “butchered lumps of meat.”<br /><br />There is no doubt that Human Acts has aggressive goals: evaluating the macrocosm of trauma as opposed to analyzing it on a smaller one-on-one scale. I, as a reader, was affected more deeply by the mother’s story than, say, the editor’s story on the crackdown on literature. In a prologue, Han Kang’s excellent translator, Deborah Smith, states that this book is “A reminder of the human acts of which we are all capable, the brutal, and the tender, the base and the sublime.” Although the balance weighs to the brutal and base, there is just enough of the tender and sublime to provide hope. 4.5 stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12352190239065849424" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1871275072" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1871275072" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1871275072?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=korean">korean</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1871275072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6936961677399256554">I know a man from Gwangju. He is a gentle man, soft-spoken. I have watched him swaying a baby in his arms, tirelessly, for hours. He lives here now, in the United States; he has done well here - but he speaks with pride of South Korea.<br/><br/>The man from Gwangju would have still been there at the time of the Uprising, not leaving until two years later. He would have performed his obligatory military service, but that would have ended long before the rebellion of May, 1980. <br/><br/>I learned these sparse detail</span>
  <span id="freeText6936961677399256554" style="display:none">I know a man from Gwangju. He is a gentle man, soft-spoken. I have watched him swaying a baby in his arms, tirelessly, for hours. He lives here now, in the United States; he has done well here - but he speaks with pride of South Korea.<br /><br />The man from Gwangju would have still been there at the time of the Uprising, not leaving until two years later. He would have performed his obligatory military service, but that would have ended long before the rebellion of May, 1980. <br /><br />I learned these sparse details about the man from Gwangju just now, as I was reading this novel. And now I wonder what he saw, what he felt, 37 years ago. <br /><br />Gwangju, there in the south, was known for its dissent. There, a democratization movement began, grew. Students, of course, but also factory workers. So it was a labor movement, too. Martial law was the governmental response. Then more protests. Then Special Forces came and eventually they opened fire. Two hundred died, but that mere number ignores the brutality, the torture, the sadism.<br /><br />The justification was a fear of Communism from the North. It required the United States' consent to move government forces from the DMZ to Gwangju. The United States consented. President Reagan, it is said, strongly endorsed the action.<br /><br />Strongmen and 10,000 banners. Children armed with guns. Twenty-six police died, too. A novel and Wikipedia. What to make of these events?<br /><br />Well, I know a man from Gwangju . . .</span>
  <a data-text-id="6936961677399256554" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1583704712" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1583704712" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1583704712?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846275968" href="/book/show/28172483-human-acts">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Aubrey by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn">Bjorn</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-everything">person-of-everything</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-translated">reality-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-reality-translated">person-of-reality-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-translated">antidote-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-translated">person-of-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1583704712" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12208199556116454390"><blockquote>
  <i>No one had ever taught me how to address a person's soul.</i>
</blockquote>If one is to take the path of see no evil/hear no evil/speak no evil when it comes to the relationship between writer and writing, the consideration of bodies in conjunction with paper and pen and keyboard and the electricity of nerve signals is cut off at the root. That's fine, though. Then you don't need to think about all that must be involved when literature is a thing of capitalism, the funding for the composition and the funding </span>
  <span id="freeText12208199556116454390" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>No one had ever taught me how to address a person's soul.</i>
</blockquote>If one is to take the path of see no evil/hear no evil/speak no evil when it comes to the relationship between writer and writing, the consideration of bodies in conjunction with paper and pen and keyboard and the electricity of nerve signals is cut off at the root. That's fine, though. Then you don't need to think about all that must be involved when literature is a thing of capitalism, the funding for the composition and the funding for the translation and the funding for the spotlight, spotlight, this is what the money wants you to hear. Some have a smaller audience and some have a harsher message and some even deal with that dangerous topic of humans being civilized enough to live within social constructs that mow them down with machine guns at will, but one does not need to translate Korean literature into English to find unmarked graves in one's backyard. All of this is to show how a little amputation goes a long way, and the myopic compromises you make with literature as a whole render you corrupt when it comes to treating with Han Kang and this book you hold in your hands. You could be offended at this, but that doesn't change the fact that you can't be trusted.<blockquote>
  <i>In the quiet corners of your conscious mind, memories are waiting. What they call forth cannot strictly be called nightmares.</i>
</blockquote>Students are slaughtered the world over. Women are gang raped the world over. Corpses are burned in militarized secrecy the world over. I gave both this and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1447640.The_Guest" title="The Guest by Hwang Sok-yong" rel="nofollow noopener">The Guest</a> five stars, but I'm more comfortable with the latter's top-rated status because of the vague feeling that it constitutes less as propaganda. Propaganda? Within the context of Korea and Korean and the Democratic Republic and the Republic and diaspora, no. That is not a judgment call I have the right to make. But when specifically chosen for not only translation into the lingo of the current hegemony but this English PEN Award business with its all encompassing threat of "freedom of expression" tied into its modus operandi, yeah. The award's website characterizes English as "the global language of power" that will solve the "complex and unequal world", which, hello? Used to readers who don't actually read, are we? So, those of you in the audience who are engaging with this piece through "the global language of power" in expectation of solving the "complex and unequal world": if all you're left with is catharsis and gratitude for not living in Korea, all truth is a matter of points of view, but some views have higher body counts, and we haven't yet figured out how to revive the dead.<blockquote>
  <i>You want me to tell you about these dead kids, professor? Like felled trees, lying in such an unnaturally straight line.<br />What right do you have to demand that of me?</i>
</blockquote>As for the work itself, it's not one that's meant for me. It's strengthened my resolve to continue my pursuit of writing that delves into the chest cavity and squeezes, digs through the bloody earth for that last fragment of the body of evidence, parses the line of censorship as a gaping maw that must be burned away at all costs, but I've got enough work on the United States/Klu Klux Klan center of things without worrying about its military industrial complexes in foreign lands. If you're not Korean and you want to "help", don't. If you're not Korean and you want to pay attention, great. If you're not Korean and all you know is white privilege satires and lands where white privilege doesn't mean shit and pop music with an ethnic label and gigantic churches and kimchi, I really don't know what it means that you've found your way to reading my review. If you're ten years and up older than me, you were alive when all this went down. How long did it take you to hear about it? Does it disturb? Does it sadden? Do you take such stories for granted and continue on in intentional fallacy bliss? What exactly will the consumption of this work by a reader such as yourself achieve, and will the work have found it worth it in the end?<blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>The Cambodian government's killed another 2 million of theirs. There's nothing stopping us from doing the same.</b>
  </i>
</blockquote>The fact that I rarely remember my dreams and even more rarely remember my nightmares goes a long way in explaining why I read the things I read and write the things I write. I don't expect the same of everyone I meet, but the line between ignorance and bad faith is clearly defined. If you're going to read this work, great. If you're not going to read this work, great. If you're going to read this work and deny the humanity of everyone involved in order to use it as a metaphor, what the actual fuck.<blockquote>
  <i>Eun-sook found herself wondering why someone so timid and feeble would maintain close relationships with writers who were under the scrutiny of the authorities; why he kept on publishing precisely those books which earned the censors' attention.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="12208199556116454390" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('d2phP+Ea0GNPiopSRtjEJwL2DZr8axEuD1feiACrnigFlMKc+NDPKLtCOp/BJYTqiASqLTmUfodHY48o/mAHGw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vU7Te10KgDgkgDFuORe6uZ69sRKIpJfXZeVvjJtvxqnPsHDYRMCfc9BIgaO+6vp0FE8WpU1b+H4t0T4sZaRfmg==')})
  }

He does not have friends—the two<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2016269975434521496\" style=\"display:none\">This story is, in short, about a monster meeting another monster. \n\nOne of the monsters is me.\n\nYunjae was born with a brain condition called Alexithymia that makes it hard for him to feel emotions like fear or anger. He does not have friends—the two almond-shaped neurons located deep in his brain have seen to that—but his devoted mother and grandmother aren’t fazed by his condition. Their little home above his mother’s used bookstore is decorated with colorful post-it notes that remind him when to smile, when to say &quot;thank you,&quot; and when to laugh. Yunjae grows up content, even happy, with his small family in this quiet, peaceful space.\n\nThen on Christmas Eve—Yunjae’s sixteenth birthday—everything changes. A shocking act of random violence shatters his world, leaving him alone and on his own. Struggling to cope with his loss, Yunjae retreats into silent isolation, until troubled teenager Gon arrives at his school and begins to bully Yunjae. \n\nAgainst all odds, tormentor and victim learn they have more in common than they realized. Gon is stumped by Yunjae’s impassive calm, while Yunjae thinks if he gets to know the hotheaded Gon, he might learn how to experience true feelings. Drawn by curiosity, the two strike up a surprising friendship. As Yunjae begins to open his life to new people—including a girl at school—something slowly changes inside him. And when Gon suddenly finds his life in danger, it is Yunjae who will step outside of every comfort zone he has created to perhaps become a most unlikely hero. \n\nThe Emissary meets The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Nighttime in this poignant and triumphant story about how love, friendship, and persistence can change a life forever.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2016269975434521496\" First published in 1978, it speaks to the painful social costs of reckless industrialization, even as it tellingly portrays the spiritual malaise of the newly rich and powerful and a working class subject to forces beyond its control. Cho\'s lean, clipped, deceptively simple style, the rapidly shifting points of view, terse dialogue, and subtle irony evoke the particularities of life in 1970s South Korea in the presence of global economic forces.\n\n\n\nThe desperate realities of life for the dwarf, the proverbial little guy upon whose back Korea\'s economic transformation largely took place, are emotively rendered in twelve linked stories examining the lives of a laboring family, a family of the newly emerging middle class, and that of a wealthy industrialist. The stories have overlapping characters and situations: the murder of a swindler, a family\'s eviction from a squatter settlement, the assassination of an important executive, the dwarf \'s fantasy of <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9212438862575553433">소설가 <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18166474.___' title=' 한강' rel='nofollow noopener'> 한강</a><br/><br/>Han Kang is the daughter of novelist Han Seung-won. She was born in Kwangju and at the age of 10, moved to Suyuri (which she speaks of affectionately in her work "Greek Lessons") in Seoul. <br/><br/>She studied Korean literature at Yonsei University. She began her writing career when one of her poems was featured in the winter issue of the quarterly Literature and Society. She made her official liter</span>
  <span id="freeText9212438862575553433" style="display:none">소설가 <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18166474.___" title=" 한강" rel="nofollow noopener"> 한강</a><br /><br />Han Kang is the daughter of novelist Han Seung-won. She was born in Kwangju and at the age of 10, moved to Suyuri (which she speaks of affectionately in her work "Greek Lessons") in Seoul. <br /><br />She studied Korean literature at Yonsei University. She began her writing career when one of her poems was featured in the winter issue of the quarterly Literature and Society. She made her official literary debut in the following year when her short story "The Scarlet Anchor" was the winning entry in the daily Seoul Shinmun spring literary contest. <br /><br />Since then, she has gone on to win the Yi Sang Literary Prize (2005), Today's Young Artist Award, and the Korean Literature Novel Award. As of summer 2013, Han teaches creative writing at the Seoul Institute of the Arts while writing stories and novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9212438862575553433" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
